4-4- Sound measurement 
Before conducting tests, sound waves could be recorded and use for classification of 
samples and calculate some dynamic modulus such as P-wave modulus. Propagation 
velocity of primary wave and shear wave of samples could be calculated. Velocity in this 
concept defines as propagation distance of wave through the sample length divided by 
wave propagation time. Shear velocity is an average value of shear wave 1 velocity and 
shear wave 2 velocity .Definitions of primary and shear waves are explained in section 2-6. 
As mentioned before, the velocity is defined as distance of propagated wave through the 
sample length divided by wave propagation time which is formulated as follows: 
𝑉=
𝐿𝑒𝑛𝑔ℎ𝑡 𝑜𝑓 𝑠𝑎𝑚𝑝𝑙𝑒
𝑤𝑎𝑣𝑒 𝑝𝑟𝑜𝑝𝑎𝑔𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒−𝐷𝑒𝑙𝑎𝑦 𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒 
65 
In the equation 65 the length of sample is known and the wave propagation time could be 
obtained from the 𝑉−𝑡 diagram. This diagram could be plotted based on the recorded 
sound waves. In this set of data, the wave propagation velocity and also the wave 
propagation time are existed. Propagation velocity and propagation time are plotted 
against each other for P-wave, S1-Wave and S2-wave.The wave propagation time is 
estimated from this plot. Usually primary wave is read as a first drop in the 𝑉−𝑡  plot and 
shear wave is read when the effects of primary wave disappear, which this point is known 
as a zero crossing. All waves are recorded at 1.4𝑀𝑃𝑎 stress level. At such a low stress level 
the shear waves are recorded very badly because the receiving waves are too weak.  
In the Figure 35-Figure 37 red crosses show the wave propagation time for each sample.

88

Figure 36: shear 1wave propagation time for sample V1

Delay time could be measured by using the billets, which are mentioned in section 3-2-4-1- 
System delay measurement, Table 3. These billets are homogenous, so the sound wave is 
not affected due to the fractured inside a sample. Therefore, the same sound wave which is 
transferred will be received. For the mentioned billets the sound waves at stresses

According to the trend line in Figure 39 the estimated shear wave velocity in steel 
is 2740 𝑚/𝑠. The difference between the measured and theoretical velocity is 15.3%. 
Dispersion e indicates that recorded wave is being influenced by other waves either 
directly or through reflections on the surface of the pistons. This influence is result of a 
complex process which can reduce one's ability to interpret the arrival of shear waves 
(CAMACHO-TAUTA, et al., 2012).

Table 13 confirms primary wave in the expected range based on the previous studies. 
However, shear wave values are below the predicted values. Therefore, the shear wave 
velocity values are not reliable to be used in further calculation. The cause of incorrect 
values for shear waves is in the sound measurement. Usually in low stress 
levels (𝑠𝑢𝑐ℎ 1.4𝑀𝑃𝑎) recorded signals of shear waves is very weak based on author’s 
==PAGE:106==
95 
 
experience. The reason is poor connection between surface of pistons (sensors) and 
specimen, which also happened when shear wave for sandstone samples were 
recording (𝜎𝑎= 1𝑀𝑃𝑎) . 𝑉𝑃−𝑉𝑠 Plot should represent linear behavior for primary and 
shear wave, the slope of such a behavior is about 0.35 (Gary Mavko, 2003): 
Mavko study 
 
 
Figure 40: Left:  𝐕𝐏−𝐕𝐬 plot for Mavko study, Right: 𝐕𝐏−𝐕𝐬 plot for different direction in this study  
Figure 40 shows the difference between results of Mavko et al. study and this project. The 
slope for vertical direction and horizontal2 direction is negative which is wrong. However 
horizontal1’s slope is close to the Mavko et al. study. As it can be observed in the Figure 40 
the recorded values are scattered remarkably while in the mentioned study there is no 
scattering. 
On the one hand 𝑉𝑠 values are not reliable and should not be taken into the account. 
Comparing of 𝑉𝑠 values of this project with mentioned studies as well as comparison of 
𝑉𝑃−𝑉𝑠  Plot, prove this fact. on the other hand 𝑉𝑠 values represent a result as expected. 
According to Figure 41 primary wave velocity propagates rather in high porosity medium 
slower than low porosity medium. This behavior of primary wave is also obtained by 
Mavko et al., Fabricius et al. and Olsen et al. (Gary Mavko, 2003; Ida L. Fabricius, 2010; 
Casper Olsen, 2008)   
𝑉𝑃− Plot is only illustrated for 75𝑚𝑚 sampels. As mentioned before porosity 
measurement for 19.05 𝑚𝑚 samples is carried out with an error due to the use of extra 
billets for filling empty volume in matrix (see Picture 3). Also based on equation 65 velocity 
==PAGE:107==
96 
 
has a direct proportion of the length, which means when the sample length decreases the 
velocity will decrease as well. Primary velocities for 19.05𝑚𝑚 samples are reported in 
Table 10, Table 11 and Table 12. These values did not behave as expected .Primary 
velocities for small samples are same or higher that primary velocities for 75 𝑚𝑚 samples. 
Based on these two reasons primary velocities are neglected for plot against their 
porosities      
 
Figure 41: 𝐕𝐏−∅ for all directions 
According to the Figure 41, the parameter called critical porosity (𝑐) can be seen. There is 
critical porosity for most of the porous materials. 𝑐 divides mechanical and acoustic 
properties of material to two different domains. Materials could carry a load with porosity 
lower than critical porosity whereas materials with porosity greater than critical porosity 
easily fall apart and suspend (Gary Mavko, 2003). Critical porosity is estimated from the 
point where the trend lines of primary velocity intercept the porosity axis (Casper Olsen, 
2008).  
Critical porosities of saturated chalk in this project are 53.73%, 62.10% and 87.25% for 
vertical, Horizontal1 and Horizontal2 directions. Andersson (1999) foundd critical porosity 
of 60%  for chalk, Fabricius et al. (2003) achieved 𝑐 of 60% −75%  and Olsen et al. (2008)

4-5- Tests 
All tests are done on chalk samples taken from Stevns area which are fully saturated. Tests 
are carried out at room temperature.    
4-5-1-Brazilian Test 
In Brazilian test load is applied on samples continuously. Loading rate is set at 0.8 𝐾𝑁 𝑚𝑖𝑛
⁄
 
, which cause samples’ failure within 15 −30 𝑠𝑒𝑐. Brazilian test is the simple test, which 
could measure tensile strength of samples. Tensile strength is formulated in equation 13 
Failure point is estimated bashed on the 𝑙𝑜𝑎𝑑−𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒 plot that is obtained during test 
running. As mentioned before tensile failure point is a point when a crack is observed on 
the samples. In the 𝑙𝑜𝑎𝑑−𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒 plot the first drop represents this point. Figure 42 
illustrates 𝑙𝑜𝑎𝑑−𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒 plot for sample V1; red Cross shows the tensile failure point load. 
The calculated tensile strength for this sample is reported as 𝜎𝑡 for horizontal2 direction.

According to above information, average values of indirect tensile strength for vertical, 
horizontal1 and horizontal2 directions are 0.33𝑀𝑃𝑎, 0.32 𝑀𝑃𝑎 and 0.34 𝑀𝑃𝑎 respectively. 
There is no significant change in tensile strength for different directions it which means 
tensile strength is not sensitive to the diversity of directions. This lack of sensitivity is also 
reported by R.I.Korsnes et al. (R.I. Korsnes, 2008) 
==PAGE:110==
99 
 
 
Figure 43: Tensile strength for different direction and average their average values 
Figure 43 illustrates tensile strength for vertical, horizontal1 and horizontal2 directions. 
Lines in this plot show the average values of tensile strength for stated directions. Tensile 
strength for this study is lower than other studies which are done by other researcher. For 
example Korsnes reported tensile strength values between 0.58𝑀𝑃𝑎−0.54𝑀𝑃𝑎 for water 
saturated Stevns chalk (R.I. Korsnes, 2008). Risnes et al. reported tensile strength for 
saturated Aalborg chalk and saturated Liege chalk 0.53 𝑀𝑃𝑎 and 0.56 𝑀𝑃𝑎 respectively. (R. 
Risnes, 2000)    
Brazilian Test is run on samples with close porosity to each other as minimize effect of 
porosity on strength and other mechanical properties. Generally strength of a sample with 
higher porosity shows lower strength value and vice versa generally. This fact is supported 
by experiment conducted by R.Risnes et al. in different papers, Risnes ran Brazilian test on 
water saturated chalk from Ekofisk field, liege chalk and limestone (R. Risnes, 2000) (R. 
RISNES, 1999). In all three cases, high porosity specimens have a low tensile strength. 
However in this study stated relation is not fully met for sample test. Following figures 
show how indirect tensile strength changes regarding to porosity.

Figure 44 shows one set of samples with same low porosity that has higher indirect tensile 
strength in comparison with other set of high porosity samples. Trend line shows inverse 
relation between porosity and indirect tensile strength. However, V03 and V05 samples do 
not represent desired trend. Inverse relation between indirect tensile strength and 
porosity for H101, H102 and H103 samples are fully supported. However, as illustrates in 
Figure 45, H105 sample represents unexpected behavior. Samples in Horizontal2 direction 
(see Figure 46) shows direct relation between indirect tensile strength and porosity. This 
unexpected result could be explained by error in porosity measurement for Brazilian 
sample. Error in porosity measurement could similarly effects V03, V05 and H105 samples. 
As mentioned in pervious section 4-4- Sound measurement, the primary wave velocities 
are measured for Brazilian samples. Primary wave velocities are represented in Table 14-
Table 16. Figure 41 illustrates porosity as inversely proportional to primary wave velocity.

The porosities of specimens in UCS test are different from each other within ±0.5%. As 
mentioned before, samples with close porosity to each other are selected in order to 
minimize effect of porosity on strength and other mechanical properties. If porosity plots 
against uniaxial compressive strength and tangent Young’s modulus, absence of porosity 
effect on mentioned parameters should be observed as follows: 
 
Figure 52: Effect of porosity on uniaxial compressive strength and tangent Young’s modulus, UCS test 
In Figure 52 as predicted difference in porosity has slight effects on 𝐶0 and 𝐸𝑡 or in some 
case has no effect. For vertical samples, Only unexpected behavior is observed, where trend 
line of 𝐶0 in vertical direction by increasing porosity shows decrease. For all directions, 
trend lines show effect of porosity on tangent Young’s modulus and 𝐶0 are straight lines.  
Appendix 14 illustrates modulus of elasticity versus bulk density for samples with different 
induration. In this study, average of tangent Young’s modulus and average of bulk density 
for all three directions are lower than samples with same induration.

generally has low primary wave velocity. This tendency is observed for Young’s modulus in 
the study done by Olsen et al. for saturated chalk sample.

Samples’ result in vertical direction shows an increase tendency. However, for horizontal 
directions a slight increase could be observed. 
As stated before, uniaxial compressive strength and porosity have inverse relation. P-wave 
modulus and porosity show the same trend (Ida L. Fabricius, 2010). Based on mentioned 
relation between porosity, P-wave modulus and porosity, uniaxial compressive strength, 
the relation between porosity and P-wave modulus could be concluded. By increasing 
uniaxial compressive strength, P-wave modulus will increase as well. P-wave modulus 
shows the homogeneity of sample. 
 
Figure 55: 𝐂𝟎−𝐌 for chalk samples in different direction, UCS test 
Figure 55  shows that by increasing P-wave modulus, uniaxial compressive strength for 
vertical samples increases. However, Horizontal directions show a slight increase in 𝐶0 by 
increasing P-wave modulus. 
4-5-3-Triaxial Test  
The last test conducted in this study is Triaxial test. This test has two parts, Triaxial 
compressive test and Triaxial extension test. Data from these set of tests complete 
necessary information in order to predicting failure behavior of rocks by p −q diagram 
and Mohr- Coulomb criterion.

In Triaxial test three types of failure mode could be observed. In Triaxial compressive test 
shear failure is happened, moreover based on the axial and radial load in Triaxial extension 
test shear or tensile failure is happened. Pore collapse as the last type of failure is not taken 
into account in this study. Pore collapse usually happens in high hydrostatic stress level, 
before setting up boundary condition for Triaxal compressive and extension test. In order 
to find pore collapse failure point and prevent getting close to that point, hydrostatic test is 
conducted for some dummy saturated chalk samples in vertical and horizontal directions. 
Figure 56 shows how vertical sample behaves under hydrostatic test. 
 
 
Figure 56: Left: stress strain curve for hydrostatic test on vertical sample, Right: red cross shows pore collapse failure point 
Pore collapse failure point for dummy saturated chalk samples with 44% porosity in 
vertical and horizontal directions are 6.68 𝑀𝑃𝑎 and 7.02 𝑀𝑃𝑎 respectively. Stress-strain 
curve for horizontal dummy sample can be seen in Appendix 15 
4-5-3-1- Triaxia Compressive l test 
In this type of Triaixal test axial stress (𝜎𝑎) and radial stress (𝜎𝑟)increase to an equal 
percentage. In other words axial and radial stress is increased hydrostatically to a point. 
Next, radial stress is kept constant and axial stress will be increased to failure. For better 
prediction of rock failure behavior, two different boundary conditions are chosen for 
compressive triaxial test, which are mentioned as follows:

4-5-3-2-Triaxial Extension test 
Extension as the second type of Triaxial test is conducted. Test is started with hydrostatic 
phase up to a stress point based on the pore collapse failure point. After this phase, radial 
stress is kept constant and axial stress is decreased down to the failure. The boundary 
conditions in Triaxial Extension test are implemented as follows:  
First boundary condition →𝜎𝑎= 𝜎𝑟= 3 𝑀𝑃𝑎 
𝜎2 = 𝜎3 = 𝜎𝑟= 3 𝑀𝑃𝑎 and 𝜎1 = 𝜎𝑎= 𝐷𝑒𝑐𝑟𝑎𝑠𝑒 𝑑𝑜𝑤𝑛 𝑡𝑜 𝑓𝑎𝑖𝑙𝑢𝑟𝑒 
69 
Second boundary condition→𝜎𝑎= 𝜎𝑟= 5 𝑀𝑃𝑎

A stress-strain diagram for Triaxial extension test looks like Figure 62. Path AB represents 
Hydrostatic phase. In this path bulk modulus can be calculated. After point B, Triaxial 
extension test is started (BCD path). Point C represents the failure point where the ideal 
plastic deformation is happened. One over slope of 50% of axial stress for BC path in stress-
strain curve shows tangent Young’s modulus. (Rasmus Risnes, 1996).

Table 25: Horizontal 2 samples 
Table 20-Table 22 represent that by increasing radial stress, axial failure points in Triaxial 
compressive test are increased.  This tendency is also observe in Triaxial extension test. In 
other words, strength of samples grows by increasing radial stress. R.Risnes et al. reported 
the same trend for saturated chalk sample with porosity around 40%. (Rasmus Risnes, 
1996). 
The tendency in Figure 63, signifies that by increasing axial and radial stress, Tangent 
Young’s decrease. However this trend differs from the result of K. Suzuki’s study. He shows 
in triaxial extension test, Tangent Young’s modulus increase by increasing radial stress. 
(Marek Kwaśniewski, 2013) . This unexpected behavior could be the result of an error in 
LVDTs’ measurement. Average of Tangent Young’s modulus for vertical, Horizontal 1 and 
Horizontal 2 are 3.31 𝐺𝑃𝑎 , 2.89 𝐺𝑃𝑎 and 2.68 𝐺𝑃𝑎 respectively.  
==PAGE:128==
117 
 
 
Figure 63: Tangent Young’s modulus-Axial failure stress for all directions 
Tangent Young’s modulus show growth in comparison with Tangent Young’s modulus of 
Triaxial compressive test, but S.A. Mofiz et al.’s  study shows that Tangent Young’s modulus 
in Triaxial compressive test are higher than Triaxial extension test. (S.A. Mofiz, 2010).  
For bulk modulus in different radial stress, no significant change could be observed. 
Although in horizontal1 direction, by increasing porosity, bulk modulus show decrease, in 
vertical direction samples have same porosity and same bulk modulus. Bulk moduli in 
Horizontal2 direction are not behaved as expected and by increasing porosity insignificant 
increase in bulk moduli is shown. Bulk modulus and porosity are inversely proportional to 
each other. (Munro, 2001) Figure 64 illustrates relation between porosity and bulk 
modulus.

4-6- Analysis of data from failure tests 
In this project Brazilian Test, uniaxial compressive strength test, Triaxial compressive test 
and Triaxial extension test are performed in order to interpret failure behavior of Stevns 
chalk, by 𝑝−𝑞 diagram and Mohr-Coulomb plot. In Brazilian test and UCS test, 
 𝑇0 and 𝐶0 are obtained as well as failure points for and Triaxial compressive and extension 
test. Before starting to analyze the failure behavior, some remarks should be mentioned. 
Direct tensile strength’s data is used for analyzing failure behavior of chalk. Direct  𝑇0 is 
calculated through the Brazilian test, which measures indirect 𝑇0. In Brazilian test and UCS 
test, intermediate and minimum principal stress are equal to zero while in Triaxial tests 
intermediate principal stress is equal to minimum principal stress. Principal stresses in this 
study are assumed as bellow: 
𝜎1: maximum principal stress 
𝜎2 ∶ Intermediate principal stress 
𝜎3 : minimum principal stress 
→𝜎1 > 𝜎2 > 𝜎3 
71 
According to mentioned assumption, the 𝑝−𝑞 diagram and Mohr-Coulomb criterion are 
demonstrated in following section and further used for analyzing the Stevns chalk samples.

values and principal stresses are reported separately for each test as bellows:

tendency. Samples in vertical direction are weaker than horizontal directions.

chalk in this study are lower than Leige’s chalk. It means in extension test Stevns chalk is 
weaker than Leige chalk.

Table 42: Result from Mohr- Coulomb diagram for extension and compaction tests 
Cohesions and friction angles which are achieved from Mohr-Coulomb diagram show 
considerable similarity to achieved cohesion and friction angle from 𝑝−𝑞 diagram with 
negligible difference.  Cohesion and friction angle values in compressive tests based on the 
Mohr-Coulomb diagram are same as 𝑝−𝑞 diagram. However, in extension test, 
Horizontal2 Direction shows an increase in cohesion and a decrease in friction angel in 
comparison with achieved cohesion and friction angle from 𝑝−𝑞 diagram. This 
unexpected behavior could be explained by an error in the sketched trend line of 𝑝−
𝑞 diagram. Dispersion of data points of extension tests in horizontal2 direction in 𝑝−
𝑞 diagram is make uncertainty in slope and interception. 
When applying an increasing level of confining stress it can be shown through a statistical 
analysis, that the strength of the material can be assumed to increase linearly as is stated 
by the Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion. Linear regression analysis has shown that this is 
indeed the case for the Stevn's chalk (Lindskov, 2015). This is also relatively obvious from 
Figure 68-Figure 70 and the statistical analysis qualifies this visual observation although 
more data points are needed in order to reach a higher certainty of a significant result. 
Few data points = low significant result 
Many data points = high significant result 
There are not a lot of data points for Triaxial tests, so the significance level is ratherlow 
(the more points we have, the more assure of our conclusion we would be). 
==PAGE:140==
129 
 
Strength of sample in extension tests are lower than compaction tests. This behavior is also 
observed in 𝑝−𝑞 diagram. 
Cohesion and friction angle for compressive test based on the Mohr-Coulomb diagram are 
reported as  30.5° and 1.12 𝑀𝑃𝑎 for saturated Aalborg chalk (Merete Vadla Madland, 
2002).Water saturated stevns chalk in this study shows lower values for cohesion and 
friction angle in compressive tests. It means samples in this study are weaker than samples 
in Madland study. This behavior of water saturated Stevns chalk in this study is also 
observed for 𝑆0 and 𝜑 values which are calculated based on 𝑝−𝑞 diagram; however, 
differs from Liege (Merete Vadla Madland, 2002) and Stvens chalk (K. Hedegaard, 2011) 
samples.   
4-6-3- Three dimension failure criteria 
In this project failure criteria by Mohr-Coulomb criterion and Modified Lade criterion are 
studied. Failure criteria are calculated for compressive and extension test in vertical, 
Horizontal1 and horizontal2 directions. 𝜋−𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑛𝑒 cross section is plotted assuming 
𝜎̅ = 2 𝑀𝑃𝑎 for both criterion. Cohesion and friction angle values, which are obtained from 
𝑝−𝑞 plot (Table 38) are used to calculate general function 𝑓1 and 𝑓2. The procedure of 
plotting is explained for each criterion as follows: 
4-6-3-1- Mohr-Coulomb criteria  
For plotting 𝜋−𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑛𝑒 based on Mohr coulomb criteria, first uniaxial compressive strength 
is calculated (equation 36 in theory). By knowing 𝐶0 general functions 𝑓1 and 𝑓2 are 
calculated based on equations 34 and 35 in theory’s section. Values of Load angle (𝜗) for 
compressive is 0° and for extension is 60° . Finally based on general functions ( 𝑓1 and 𝑓2) 
radial distance can be calculated. All calculations results are summarized in Table 43and

4-6-3-2- Modified Lade criteria  
The modified Lade criterion is plotted based on the role of intermediate principal 
stress (𝜎2).  In extension and compressive tests, 𝜎2 is not taken into account and assumed 
to be equal to 𝜎3 . Therefore, values of cohesion and friction angle between compressive 
and extension test cannot be predicted. These values are guessed and calculated by linear 
interpolation of the cohesion and friction angle for compressive and extension tests. 
For calculation of radial distance equations 28 - 31 are used. The rest of calculations to 
plot 𝜋−𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑛𝑒 cross section are in Appendix 18. Figure 72 illustrates 𝜋−𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑛𝑒 for 
Modified Lade criteria. 
 
Figure 72: Cross section of the Modified Lade criterion in a  𝛑−𝐩𝐥𝐚𝐧𝐞 
Cross section for Modified Lade has a convex shape. However in this study some data 
points are guessed and as a result in extension tests convex shape is squeezed.

4-7-Scanning Electron Microscope 
In order to gain some qualitative information about Stevns chalk in this study, SEM images 
are captured. Images are used to study about pore distribution in vertical, Horizontal1 and 
Horizontal2 directions. SEM images are also used to evaluate the pore distribution after 
failure and see if any important changes are happened. 
As mentioned before intact samples for SEM are prepared while samples are perpendicular 
to the directions’ axis. However, prepared failed samples are perpendicular to the axial 
load.

directions.

Picture 36: SEM images for intact samples 
One type of porosity in vertical direction is mostly between particles (interparticle), the 
other type of porosity, which is observed in these images is between individual grains 
(intraparticle). In horizontal samples in addition to the mentioned primary porosity, some 
equant shape pores (white arrows in horizontal 2 picture) called voggy porosity, and some 
pores that their grains have been removed (white arrows in Horizontal2) known as Moldic 
porosity are also detected. (Patsoules, 1983) Pictures in all directions illustrate that 
samples have an undulated surface. Picture 37 represents iterparticle and intraparticle 
porosity clearly. 
==PAGE:145==
134 
 
 
Picture 37: interparticle and intraparticle Porosity 
White arrows in Picture 37 show the intraparticle porosity and black colors represent 
interparticle porosity. White arrows also indicate the coccolith. Coccolith is a ring or disk 
shape calcium carbonate formed by single-celled algae. Coccolith is found too much in the 
content of chalk. 
Samples which are failed under UCS, Triaxial Compressive and Triaxial extension tests 
clearly display lamination in samples. Lamination is occurred perpendicular to the 
direction of loading. Sample which is failed under extension test shows lamination in two 
directions. Extension Triaxial test in this study has the highest axial and radial stress, which 
could be the reason why lamination is observed in two directions in Triaxial extension. 
Mahbaz et al. observed the same phenomenon in his study even though only one test is run 
by applying axial load on samples (Mahbaz, 2013). Following picture illustrates lamination 
in mentioned tests.

5- Conclusion 
In this project the main results are summarized as follows: 
1- Porosity is a function of rock strength, in order to minimize effects of porosity on 
strength and other mechanical properties, samples with close porosity to each other are 
chosen. Absence of porosity’s effects is demonstrated based on the Kozeny’s Equation and 
Strength-Porosity plots for Brazilian and UCS tests. 
2- Porosity averages and primary wave averages are the same in three directions. However, 
permeability average in Vertical direction is lower than Horizontal directions. 
3- Tensile strength is directly proportional to primary wave velocity. Primary wave velocity 
increases by increasing tensile strength 
4- Primary wave velocity and p-wave modulus are increased by increasing compressive 
strength. But, primary waves are increased while Tangent Young’s moduli are decreased. 
5- In Triaxial compressive test Tangent Young’s moduli are increased by increasing axial 
and radial stress. 
6- In Triaxial Extension test Tangent Young’s moduli are decreased by increasing axial and 
radial stress. 
7- Tengent Young’s moduli in Triaxial extension are higher than Triaxial compressive test. 
However, this relation should be vice versa. 
8- Bulk moduli in Triaxial extension are lower than bulk moduli in Triaxial compressive 
test, but this relation should be vice versa 
9- Based on the experimental results, Tensile strength has isotropic behavior. However, 
compressive strength in UCS test, failure axial points in Triaxial extension and Triaxial 
compressive test, display anisotropic behavior. 
10- Analyzing of samples based on failure lines in Mohr-Coulomb and p-q diagram show 
that strength of samples in compressive tests are higher than extension tests. 
==PAGE:148==
137 
 
11- There is a considerable similarity between estimated cohesion and friction angle values 
in Mohr-Coulomb diagram and p-q diagram. 
12- Based on the SEM images, Interparticle, Intraparticle, Voggy and Moldic porosities are 
detected. Also SEM pictures show lamination in failed samples except samples which are 
failed in Brazilian test.
